**CONTRIBUTION OF THE JĪYARS OF ŚRĪ AHOBILA MAṬHA TO ŚRĪVAIṢṆAVA PHILOSOPHY AND RELIGION**

**THESIS SUBMITTED**
**TO THE UNIVERSITY OF MADRAS**
**FOR THE AWARD OF THE DEGREE OF**
**DOCTOR OF PHILOSOPHY IN VAISHNAVISM**

**RESEARCH SCHOLAR:**
MRS. K. ANURADHA, M.A.
Department of Vaishnavism
University of Madras
Madras - 600 005

**RESEARCH SUPERVISOR:**
DR. M. NARASIMHACHARY
Professor & Head
Department of Vaishnavism
University of Madras.
Madras - 600 005

**DEPARTMENT OF VAISHNAVISM**
**UNIVERSITY OF MADRAS**
**MADRAS 600 005**

**JANUARY 1997**
[]
## 
### Preface

The Ahobila Maṭha is a Śrīvaiṣṇava centre of great fame and antiquity. It will be 600 years old in 1998. It derives the greatness from the presiding deity of the Maṭha, Śrī Mālola Nṛsiṁha, one of the nine Narasiṁhas atop the Ahobilam Hill, forming part of the Nallamalai ranges of Kurnool District in Andhra Pradesh. It is one of the 108 divyadeśas, sacred to Śrīvaiṣṇavas. The Āḻvārs have sung the glory of this holy place in their Divyaprabandha. The place has gained sanctity, because according to the scriptures (Brahmāṇḍa Purāṇa) this is the very place where Lord Narasiṁha manifested himself and killed the demon King Hiraṇyakaśipu.

During the six centuries of its existence, since its inception in 1398 A.D. when Kiḍāmbi Śrīnivāsācārya of Tirunārāyaṇapuram, a young man hardly twenty years of age, was inspired by Śrī Lakṣmī Nṛsiṁha to renounce the world for propagating Rāmānuja Darśana to the present day, the Ahobila Maṭha has been headed by eminent scholars, inspired poets, intrepid builders and noted dramatists. The present pontifical head Śrīman Nārāyaṇa Yatīndra Mahādeśika is 45th in the line. Carrying the mantle of his forbears in propagating the Śrīvaiṣṇava religion and philosophy handed down by the God-intoxicated Āḻvārs and the benign Ācāryas, His Holiness demonstrates how spirituality and
[]
monasticism can be effectively practised in a secular society. The propagation of *Rāmānuja Darśana* must go hand in hand with projecting the arcā worship of the Lord and the other great Ācāryas for encompassing the devotees into its fold. It is in this background that the contribution of the Jīyars of the Ahobila Maṭha gains in significance and has been chosen as the subject of the present Ph.D. programme.

The thesis is divided into five chapters. The first chapter is introductory in nature and deals with Śrīvaiṣṇavism as a religion and the basic tenets of the *Viśiṣṭādvaita* philosophy - the inseparability of Śrī and Viṣṇu, the indispensability of an Ācārya for human redemption who acts as a mediator between the suffering soul and the compassionate Lord and the greatness and glory of *Prapatti Yoga* superseding *Karma, Jñāna and Bhakti Yogas*. The Vaḍakalai (Northern sect of Śrīvaiṣṇavism) ācārya paramparā originating from Lord Nārāyaṇa upto Śrīmad Ādi Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa Yatīndra Mahādeśika, founder Jīyar of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha is also given in this chapter.

In the second chapter the Saṁnyāsa tradition is taken up for study. The duties enjoined in the saṁnyāsa āśrama according to Yādavaprakāśa's *Yatidharmasamuccaya* and the *Yatiliṅgasamarthana* of Śrī Vatsya Varadācārya have been presented. The Śrīvaiṣṇava ascetics fall into four groups - *kuṭīcaka*,
[]
*bahūdaka, haṁsa and paramahaṁsa*- each latter one being superior to the previous one. The *yajñopavīta, tridaṇḍa, śikhā, pātra and kaupīna* are the five essential things which a Śrīvaiṣṇava ascetic should always possess. He has to perform the annual *cāturmāsya vrata*, starting with the *saṅkalpa* on the *āṣāḍha pūrṇimā* day, which is termed as *saṅkalpa mahotsava*.

The third chapter is biographical in nature, dealing with the lives of the Jīyars of Ahobila Maṭha, their sojourns to the holy shrines, some of which were inaccessible, traversing the length and breadth of India even in days of yore when there were no proper means of transport, their expositions of the *grantha catuṣṭaya*, construction of temples, digging of tanks, establishment of *agrahāras*, installation of the idols of *pūrvācāryas* in temples, the construction of Bṛndāvanas for them and establishment of *Vedapāṭhaśālās*. Of special significance in recent times, is the construction of the 13-tiered *Rājagopuram* at Śrīraṅgam costing 15 million rupees, by the 44th Jīyar, when he was 89 years old. This is the tallest tower in Asia, 165 ft. in length and 150 ft. in breadth and rising upto 226 ft. in height. The present 45th Jīyar is carrying on this holy tradition by expanding the activities of Maṭha, making the forest shrines of Ahobilam accessible to all by laying roads, providing rest houses and renovating the tanks and temples at Ahobilam and providing the pleasure of having *darśana* of the *mūla* and *utsava*
[]
idols of Mālola Nṛsiṁha in unison at Ahobilam, which had not taken place for many bygone years.

The fourth chapter is devoted to the study of the compositions of the Jīyars. These can be broadly classified into philosophical, religious and literary works though there is an undercurrent of an admixture of philosophy, religion and literature in all their works. It should be mentioned here that not all the Jīyars have contributed in this direction. The works are selected on the basis of their literary and philosophical importance. Smaller *stotras* like *daśakas, aṣṭottaraśatas and maṅgalāśāsanas* are not taken into consideration. Some of the works are highly scholarly disputations, some are elucidatory of important concepts like *Puruṣakāra* and *Rahasyas*, while some others are commentarial, expounding basic texts like the *Śrībhāṣya* and *Gītārthasaṅgraha*. As many as 25 works of the Pontiffs have been studied in this chapter. Some of the works are in the form of palm-leaf / paper manuscripts while a majority of them is available in print. The fact that the Pontiffs could find time to compose excellent treatises inspite of their busy schedule of ritual worship of the presiding deity Śrī Mālola Nṛsiṁha, temple renovations, periodical tours and so on, makes the study all the more inspiring and interesting.

The Jīyar of Iñjimeḍu, Śrīraṅga Śaṭhakopa, the 42nd Pontiff has also left his mark in the annals of the Maṭha by establishing Vedapāṭhaśālā, Prabhandha
[]
*pāṭhaśālā*, a Sanskrit College at Madurāntakam, bringing out the monthly Publication of the Maṭha, *Śrī Nṛsiṁha Priyā*, and conducting the annual Mālola Vidvatsadas where scholars in Vedānta were called upon to air their views. This is a tradition that is still continuing to the present day and is eagerly looked forward to, by the devout Public. The present Jīyar has written a Tamil commentary on the *Viṣṇupurāṇa*. In addition, His Holiness has edited the *Śrīvaiṣṇava-Sadācāra-Nirṇaya* of Śrī Raṅganātha Yatīndra, the 26th pontiff, and the *Prapannajayantī-nirṇaya* of the 27th Jīyar, Śrī Vīrarāghava Yatīndra Mahādeśika. He is also the editor of the *Śrī Nṛsiṁha Priyā* wherein his editorial is read with avid interest by layman and scholar alike.

In conclusion, the fifth chapter estimates and sums up the mighty work of the holy saints who are spreading the message of Rāmānuja, carrying the idol of Lord Mālola in their tours for the sake of rejuvenating the masses, performing *pañcasaṁskāras* and *bharanyāsa* to the devotees, encompassing them into the Śrīvaiṣṇava fold and spreading the messages of unity and synthesis not only in the Śrīvaiṣṇava community but among those belonging to other faiths. The Jīyars not only transmit the traditional truths through such activities as publishing *Śrī Nṛsiṁha Priyā* in different languages to cater to the needs of the people living in different states, and by giving lectures/discourses, and guiding and encouraging
[]
scholarly assemblies, but also by training the young and devout disciples in the traditional lore and grooming them to be future *ācāryas* and authentic interpreters of the glorious *siddhānta* of Śrī Rāmānuja. By leading an ascetic life and expounding simple truths, they lend a new meaning to life and offer solutions for the suffering humanity and thus exude an air of harmony and peace wherever they go.

It is now my pleasant duty to acknowledge gratefully the help and cooperation I received directly and indirectly from individuals and institutions in preparing this thesis. At the outset I must express my thanks to the authorities of the University of Madras for giving me the permission to register myself as a Ph.D. research scholar with the Department of Vaishnavism in January 1992. I am also grateful to them for granting me the University Research Fellowship from January 1995 till date.

I record with deep gratitude, the help and encouragement given to me by my Research Supervisior, Dr. M. Narasimhachary, Professor and Head of the Department of Vaishnavism, University of Madras. He has liberally spared his time and energy for me, though he has been unsparing in his demand for excellence in my method and presentation. He has been a source of constant guidance and inspiration to me in the course of my research work. The study of
[]
the scholarly works of the Jīyars was made enjoyable when my *Guru* explained the subtleties in their interpretation of various texts and cleared my doubts and questions very meticulously. But for his kind and timely help, I would not have been able to complete this task.

I extend my thanks to Dr. V.K.S.N. Raghavan, Professor, Department of Vaishnavism, University of Madras, for the personal interest he took in my academic progress. I am also thankful to Dr. M.A. Venkatakrishnan, Reader, Department of Vaishnavism, University of Madras, who has been helpful in completing my thesis. I would also like to thank Sri Ranganathan, Vaishnavism Department, University of Madras, for his kind help.

I must express my thanks to the Curator and staff, Government Oriental Manuscript Library, Madras for permitting me to use the library and provide necessary references from Catalogues and Journals. I would like to thank Mrs. Rajalakshmi in particular for helping me in my reference work there. My grateful thanks are also due to the Director, Adyar Library and Research Centre, Madras, and to the Director of the Radhakrishnan Institute for Advanced study in Philosophy, University of Madras, for permitting me to use their libraries in connection with my research work.
[]
I record with gratitude, the timely help rendered by Mrs. Kasthuri Raghavan and Master Mukundan, in typing the thesis neatly and accurately.

I will be failing in my duty if I do not record my deep sense of gratitude to my family members for the constant encouragement and help given to me with perfect understanding.

Last but not the least, I wish to record my respectful homage to His Holiness the 45th Jīyar of Ahobila Maṭha, who in his *pūrvāśrama*, blessed me and suggested this subject for my research work.

K. Anuradha

viii

[]
## INTRODUCTION

***Śrīvaiṣṇavism***

The diversity of India is tremendous; it is obvious; it lies on the surface and anybody can see it . . . But disruptive tendencies gave rise immediately to an attempt to find a synthesis. Some kind of a dream of unity has occupied the mind of India since the dawn of civilization. That unity was not conceived as something imposed from outside . . . It was something deeper and within its fold, the widest tolerance of beliefs and customs was practised and every variety acknowledged and even encouraged.[^1]

This extract from the letter written by Jawaharlal Nehru during his confinement in Ahmednagar Fort in 1940s, epitomizes the greatness of Bhāratavarṣa, deriving its strength from the unison of variegated cultures and lending it a special charm and uniqueness in the panorama of world thought. This strain of unity in diversity is the life-blood of Hinduism in general and Śrīvaiṣṇavism in particular. A detailed study of Śrīvaiṣṇava philosophy will reveal that it is this special characteristic that places Śrīvaiṣṇava system of religious philosophy, head and shoulders above any other system of thought. This fusion without division of apparently diverse elements is the very foundation of Śrī Rāmānuja Darśana i.e. the philosophy expounded by the great Śrīvaiṣṇava Ācārya, Śrī Rāmānuja.

[^1]: Jawaharlal Nehru, *The Discovery of India*, pp. 61-62, Asia Publishing House, New Delhi, 1961.
[]
The Śrīvaiṣṇava system of thought primarily implies the inseparability of Śrī and Viṣṇu. According to this system, Lord Viṣṇu is the Supreme Being and He constitutes the Godhead, along with His divine consort Goddess Lakṣmī - "the Śrī par excellence". Retaining their individual characteristics they supplement and complement each other in their *līlā* –

`अखिलभुवनजन्मस्थेमभङ्गादिलिेले`[^2]

It is rather interesting to note that the term Viśiṣṭādvaita came to be applied to this system only after Rāmānuja (1017-1137 A.D.). In fact, Rāmānuja has used the word "*Viśiṣṭādvyayaykya*". It was Sudarśanasūri (early part of 13th century) author of the celebrated commentary, the *Śrutaprakāśikā* on Rāmānuja's *Śrībhāṣya* (a commentary on Bādarāyaṇa's *Brahmasūtras*) who used "*Viśiṣṭādvaita*" for the first time.[^3] The term literally means "Qualified Non-dualism". It is in fact the Doctrine special of Pan-organismal Monotheism. Śrī Vedāntadeśika (i.e. Veṅkaṭanātha 1268-1369 A.D.) the illustrious follower of Rāmānuja explains this term in his *Nyāyasiddhāñjana* thus:

`अशेष-चिद्-अचित्-प्रकारं ब्रह्म एकमेव तत्त्वम्। तत्र प्रकार-प्रकारिणोः प्रकाराणाञ्च मिथः अत्यन्तभेदेऽपि विशिष्ट-एक्यादिविवक्षया एकत्वव्यपदेशः।`[^4]

[^2]: Benedictory verse of the *Śrībhāṣya*.
[^3]: V. Varadachari, "Antiquity of the term Viśiṣṭādvaita" in *Viśiṣṭādvaita by 24 Erudite Scholars*, Ramanuja Vedanta Society, Madras, 1976.
[^4]: part I, appendix, p.1.
[]
The Supreme Being (Brahman) is only One (Advaita). But It has all the animate and inanimate objects (*cit* and *acit*) as Its attributes. Though there is thus mutual difference between the modes and the possessor (Brahman) and between the modes themselves, the whole thing is conceived of as a complex three-in-one single unit. This concept that the entire world of men and matter form the body of the one and only supreme Brahman is the basis of Viśiṣṭādvaita philosophy. Compare the words of the English poet, Alexander Pope:

> All are but parts of one stupendous whole
> Whose body Nature is, and God, the soul.[^5]

So, philosophical knowledge consists in clearly understanding the nature of the three entities, *cit*, *acit*, and *Īśvara*, their distinctness from and also their relation to one another. These constitute the *tattvatraya* or the three Realities of Viśiṣṭādvaita. The knowledge of these entities is only a preliminary requisite to leading a life in such a way that the main purpose, specially of human life (viz. liberation from the cycle of birth and death), is achieved. The description of the means for achieving this end is termed as *hita* (viz.- *jñāna, karma, bhakti* and *prapatti*). The final goal "the consummation devoutly to be wished",[^6] namely *mokṣa* is *paramapuruṣārtha*.

[^5]: 'An Essay on Man', Epistles, Il. 267-268.
[^6]: William Shakespeare, *Complete Works, Hamlet, the Prince of Denmark*, Act. III - Sc. I, p. 1047, English Language Book Society and Collins, London and Glasgow, 1964.
[]
As a religion, Śrīvaiṣṇavism is as simple as following certain basic tenets laid down in the Vedas and the *Upaniṣads* as –

`सत्यं वद, धर्मं चर, स्वाध्यायान्मा प्रमदः।`[^7]

etc; and, as a philosophy, Viśiṣṭādvaita is as complex and capable of generating a wide variety of prismatic interpretations as is evident from the very first aphorism of the *Brahma-sūtras*, *athāto brahmajijñāsā*. But the speciality of this Viśiṣṭādvaitic Śrīvaiṣṇavism lies in the stress laid on *bhakti* or loving devotion to the Lord and *prapatti* or the doctrine of voluntary self-surrender to the Lord's Will realising fully well that —

`दासभाताः स्वतः सर्वे ह्यात्मानः परमात्मनः।`[^8]
(All selves, by nature, are subservient to the Lord)

The body is bestowed upon man (*dattam śarīram*) by God, out of His immense compasssion (*paramakāruṇya*) so that man may reach God in the end. This body in fact is a boat (*plava*), a means for crossing the ocean of worldly life (*saṁsārasāgara-nistaraṇa*). The goal viz. *sāyujyapadavī* is distant, and the means strenuous - through exercise (*abhyāsa*) and detachment (*vairāgya*). The individual has *dāsvatāntrya* i.e. independence bestowed on him to choose the right way leading to God. The yearning to reach God may germinate in a man in one life or a series of lives. By his own misdeeds he

[^7]: *Taittirīyopaniṣad*, I. 19
[^8]: *Śrī Mantrarājapadastotra*, 11.
[]
has fallen into the mire of *saṁsāra*, full of sorrows and suffering, and these act as catalysts, quickening man's desire for liberation, and thus he becomes a *mumukṣu*. The very extent of the painfulness of man's life is a pointer to the triumph that is to come in *paraloka* i.e. Vaikuṇṭha. As the Lord Himself declares: -

`यस्यानुग्रहमिच्छामि धनं तस्य हराम्यहम्।`
`बान्धवैश्च वियोगेन मूशं भवति दुःखितः॥`
`तेन दुःखेन सन्तप्तो यदि मां न परित्यजेत्।`
`तं प्रसादं करिष्यामि यत्सुरैरपि दुर्लभम्॥`[^9]

"Whomsoever I wish to make the recipient of My grace, I deprive him of his wealth and wean him away from his near and dear ones. If even in this sorrowful state, he does not abandon me, I will shower on him such a grace that even the gods would envy."

The operation of God's grace to a *mumukṣu* who through his devotion surrenders to the Lord's Will helplessly and with *mahāviśvāsa* through a spiritual *guru*, is inevitable. As is well known, *aprārthito na gopāyet*?[^10]
("protect not unless prayed for").

[^9]: *Bhāgavatapurāṇa / Varāhapurāṇa*. See *Śrīmad Rahasyatrayasāra*, p. 293, fn. 2, Pub. Śrī Deśika Sadanam, Madras, 1986.
[^10]: *Lakṣmī Tantra* (17-72). Compare Christ's words: "Ask and it shall be given; knock and the doors shall be opened".
[]
In Śrīvaiṣṇavism the role of a spiritual *guru* or *ācārya* as a mediator between the suffering soul and the compassionate Lord is extremely important for man's redemption.

`पापिष्टः क्षत्रबन्धुश्च पुण्डरीकश्च पुण्यकृत्।`
`आचार्यवत्तया मुक्तौ तस्मादाचार्यवान् भवेत्॥`[^11]

In fact the Ācārya is equated to the Lord in status —

`यथा देवे तथा गुरौ।`[^12] `देवमिवाचार्यमुपासीत।`[^13]

Madhurakavi Āḻvār states ten points[^14] of similarity between the Ācārya and the Lord. Hence it is evident that before one obtains Bhagavatsambandha, Ācāryasambandha is an absolute necessity. An Ācārya is one who grants *sadvidyā* and is *ajñānadhvāntarodhaka*. Compare the *Śrutivākya* –

`"आचार्यवान् पुरुषो वेद"`[^15]

So *ācāryavattā* is *mokṣa-kāraṇa* or better still, *mokṣa-sādhana*. For a *mumukṣu* it is essential to observe the Guruparamparā up to the Lord. It is "*Abhagavattaḥ*" i.e., originating from the Lord and not barring the Divine ones. According to the *Śrutivākya* –

`"स चाऽऽचार्यवंशो ह्येघोऽसावसाविति आभगवतः"`[^16]

[^11]: *Śrīmad Rahasyatrayasāram, Śrī Guruparamparāsāram*, p. 5.
[^12]: Cf. *Śvetāśvatara Upaniṣad*, VI. 23
[^13]: Cf. *Āpastambasūtra*, 1-6-13.
[^14]: Vide *Śrī Guruparamparāsāram*, p. 4.
[^15]: Vide Appendix to *Śrīmad Rahasyatrayasāram*, p. 3.
[^16]: Ibid. p.3.
[]
"*Asau Asau*" refers to a distant object ultimately denoting Brahman. "*Asau Asau*" also means that each and every Ācārya's Holy Name (*Tiru Nāma*) should be recited separately and hence this practice of reciting the *taniyans* (dedicatory stanzas) of Ācāryas individually. Teachers of right mind are described as those who are free from egotism (*nirahaṅkārī*), desiring others' welfare (*parasamṛddhipriyaḥ*), not attracted by love of fame or wealth (*khyātilābhanirapekṣa*). The compassionate *guru* leads the suffering soul to the Lord taking upon himself the burden of sin invested by the *mumukṣu* as *nyāsa*. This is *bharanyāsa* or *bharasamarpaṇa*.

In the Śrīvaiṣṇava *sampradāya* the first *guru* is Lord Nārāyaṇa Himself—

`तमिमं सर्वसंप्रमाचार्यं पितरं गुरुम्।`[^17] `अखिललोकानां गुरुर्नारायणो गुरुः।`[^18] `गुरुरसि गतिश्चासि जगताम्।`[^19]
`शिष्यापिताः शरण्यमिति मुमुक्षवस्त्वाम्`
`आद्यं गुरुं गुरुमरप्याधिगम्य॥`[^20]

Next only to Lord Nārāyaṇa and out of Her own will comes Śrī (personification of *dayā*) tempering the Lord's justice through Her divine mercy. This aspect of forgiveness and compassion lends a new dimension to Śrīvaiṣṇava religion as it were. It is a religion of hope, positive in its

[^17]: *Mahābhārata* : Sabhāparvan, 41-2,3.
[^18]: *Viṣṇu Purāṇa* : 5-3-14.
[^19]: Yāmuna's *Stotraratna*, 60.
[^20]: Vedāntadeśika's *Śaraṇāgatidīpikā*, 9.
[]
approach and promising redemption to the repentant erring humanity. This may be compared with the following extract: [^21]

> The quality of mercy is not strained;
> It droppeth as the gentle rain from heaven
> Upon the place beneath. It is twice blest:
> It blesseth him that gives and him that takes.
> 'Tis mightiest in the mightiest; it becomes
> The throned monarch better than his crown,
> His sceptre shows the force of temporal power,
> The attribute to awe and majesty,
> Wherein doth sit the dread and fear of things.
> But mercy is above this sceptred sway.
> It is enthroned in the heart of kings.
> It is an attribute to God Himself
> And earthly power doth then show liketh God's
> When mercy seasons justice.

Śrī is the divine Mediator called *puruṣakāra* and is co-eternal with and inseparable from the Lord (*apṛthaksiddha*) pleading for love and forgiveness. So justice and mercy are eternally blended in Him – in the Godhead Śrīman Nārāyaṇa.

Next in line comes Viṣvaksena. He is the controller of *Vaikuṇṭha* or *Paramapada*. According to tradition, he taught Nammāḻvār or Śaṭhakopa the philosophy of Śrīvaiṣṇava system.

[^21]: The famous words of Portia in Shakespeare's *Merchant of Venice*, wherein this quality of 'mercy' is extolled to heavenly heights (Act IV, Sc. l), p. 246.
[]
With this we enter a new movement called the Āḻvār movement which was to have a lasting influence in the growth of Śrīvaiṣṇavism especially in South India. The great feature of the Āḻvār movement was that it was more emotional than metaphysical. "*Āḻvār*" in Tamil means "one who is immersed". These saints were so called because they were always immersed in their love and devotion to Lord Viṣṇu. The Āḻvārs were uncompromsing in their love for and worship of Viṣṇu. The hymns of the Āḻvārs were the outbursts of God-filled souls for whom God was an intimate personal friend and guide.

The *Nālāyira-divyaprabandham* is the name by which the hymns of all the Āḻvārs came to be collectively called. The Āḻvārs were twelve in number and include a woman, the celebrated Godā or Āṇḍāḷ, to whom a magnificent temple was built in later years at Śrīvilliputtūr, her birth place.

Here, we can contain ourselves to mentioning about Nammāḻvār, the greatest of Śrīvaiṣṇava saints known by different names as Māṟan, Parāṅkuśa, Śaṭhakopa, Vakuḷābharaṇa and so on. He gave to the world four grand hymns – the *Tiruviruttam*, the *Tiruvāśiriyam*, the *Periya Tiruvan*tādi, and the *Tiruvāymoḻi*. The Śrīvaiṣṇavas call them the four Vedas in Tamil garb, and Vedāntadeśika refers to the *Tiruvāymoḻi* as the *Dramiḍopaniṣad*. Nammāḻvār's hymns have shaped the conduct and faith of the Vaiṣṇavas of South India. To the emotionally inclined these are simple appealing prayers to the different
[]
local *arcā-avatāras* of Lord Viṣṇu. To the less emotional and more philosophical, they seem to be the outcome of great learning in the *Vedas, Upaniṣads, Gītā* etc. To the practical spiritualist, they are of value as the achievement of one who was in holy communion with God. Nammāḻvār is called the "progenitor of the surrendered souls" (*prapannajana santāna kūṭastha*) by later Ācāryas of Viśiṣṭādvaita, and the fundamental doctrines of the Śrīvaiṣṇava faith, as current today, were taught by him.

After the age of the Āḻvārs (850 A.D.) we enter into a new epoch in the history of Śrīvaiṣṇavism - the age of Ācāryas. The Ācāryas expounded their system of thought based on the hymns of Āḻvārs and the ancient Sanskrit scriptures. Their object was to reconcile the *Vedas, Upaniṣads* and *Gītā* with the Tamil *Prabandhams*. In fact they aimed at interpreting the latter in terms of the former. In appreciation, posterity has conferred on them (the Ācāryas), the significant title of *Ubhayavedāntins*. They regarded the *Prabandham* as equally authoritative as the *Vedas* and introduced them in public and private worship. They widened the scope of spiritual studies so as to include Tamil scriptures. Further they elaborated and perfected the Viśiṣṭādvaita school of thought with the aid of the Doctrine of Self-surrender which they derived from the Āḻvārs. The Ācāryas were very orthodox, well-versed in Sanskrit and Tamil. They passed through the different stages of orthodoxy and discharged their *nitya* (daily) and *naimittika* (occasional) duties expected of an
[]
individual (even as the Lord Himself did in His *avatāras*) so that they may serve as standing examples to their followers. All the Śrīvaiṣṇava festivals, observances, fasts, vows and customs can be traced to the rules laid down by the Ācāryas. They were thus the makers of modern Śrīvaiṣṇavism - its society, rituals, practices and ideals. It is not therefore surprising that they, in turn, have become objects of worship and have been raised to equality with the Āḻvārs whom they themselves worshipped.

The first in this line was Nāthamuni or Śrī Raṅganātha Muni. A versatile scholar in *Vedas* and *Smṛtis*, he was a *Paramaikāntin* who enjoyed the *arcā-avatāra* of the Lord. He divided the *Prabandhams* into four parts of 1000 verses each, classified them according to different metres and introduced them to be sung in various Śrīvaiṣṇava shrines including the Śrīraṅgam temple. The installation of the images of Āḻvārs in temples and the conduct of the recitation of the *Vedas* and the *Prabandhams* alike, soon spread throughout the Śrīvaiṣṇava world.

The elevation of Tamil works to the status of the *Vedas* in the temple festivals in turn gave rise to a new and extensive type of religious literature in South India, an excellent admixture of Sanskrit and Tamil, the unique *Maṇipravāḷa*. In this intermingling of two different languages there is unity in diversity, each language retaining its individual identity and yet united in a new and appealing medium. Its main object was to expound the Tamil *Vedas*
[]
and reconcile their teachings with those of the *Prasthānatraya*. Thus the Tamil *Prabandham* became an essential part of Śrīvaiṣṇava literature. This in turn gave rise to numerous commentaries on *Prabandhams* requiring the authority of a great scholar to interpret them in the light of *Śrutis* and *Smṛtis*. This necessity, along with the need to expound and defend the Viśiṣṭādvaita system against rival schools of Śaṅkara, Madhva, and Buddha led to the establishment of the post of Ācārya "whose authority was law in religious worship and whose advice was the final word in temples and household alike." In the early Śrīvaiṣṇava period, this pontifical head was usually entrusted with the management of the Śrīraṅgam temple. The early Ācāryas were successful in their great task because of the power they wielded over the important Śrīvaiṣṇava shrines of South India. But when this combination of offices ceased, Śrīvaiṣṇavism began to be divided into sects.[^22]

It was but natural that when the dignity of the pontiff was established, the choice should fall on Nāthamuni and when he was formally appointed as the religious and administarative head in Śrīraṅgam temple, the whole Vaiṣṇava community paid homage to him. With him commenced a new era of activity and expansion in Śrīvaiṣṇavism. A great scholar and thinker, he composed two well-known works, the *Yogarahasya* and *Nyāyatattva*. None of

[^22]: V. Rangacharya, "Historical Evolution of Śrīvaiṣṇavism in South India", *Cultural Heritage of India*, IV, p.172, Pub. Sri Ramakrishna Mission Institute of Culture, Calcutta, 1969.
[]
these works is available now, though quotations from the latter are made by Vedāntadeśika "the most brilliant luminary in the history of the post-Rāmānuja Śrīvaiṣṇavism".[^23] From the references made to the *Nyāyatattva*, it appears to have been the first work in the field to interpret *Nyāya* according to the Śrīvaiṣṇava line of thought. It criticises and controverts the *Nyāyasūtras* of Gautama. Vedāntadeśika quotes several metrical and prose passages from it in his *Nyāyasiddhāñjana* and *Nyāyapariśuddhi*. The system of Viśiṣṭādvaita, as Vedāntadeśika observes in his *Tattvamuktākalāpa*, owes its origin to Nāthamuni and was developed and nurtured by Yāmuna his grandson, and protected and kept on a firm footing by Rāmānuja.

`नाथोपज्ञं प्रवृत्तं बहुभिरुपचितं यामुनेयप्रबन्धैः`
`त्रातं सम्यग्यतीन्द्रैरिदमिदमखिलं दर्शनं नः ॥`[^24]

Puṇḍarīkākṣa and Śrīrāmaṁiśra, the succeeding *ācāryas*, are not known to have composed any works, although some Taniyans (Tamil verses) are attributed to them. Then, in the Ācārya line, comes the illustrious grandson of Nāthamuni, Yāmunācārya, also known as Āḷavandār, meaning, 'one who has come to rule the spiritual world'. His compositions include (a) the *Catuślokī*, which in four verses epitomises the position of Śrī as the consort of Lord Viṣṇu and the role played by Her in the salvation of the soul,

[^23]: M. Narasimhachary, *Contribution of Yāmuna to Viśiṣṭādvaita*, p.6, Prof. M. Rangacharya Memorial Trust, Madras, 1971.
[^24]: V. 136.
[]
(b) the *Stotraratna* which in sixty-five verses praises Lakṣmīpati (Lord Viṣṇu), hints at the secret doctrine of self-surrender and highlights the *ācāryabhakti* of the author, (c) the *Gītārthasaṅgraha* which in thirty-two verses expounds in brief, the traditional teachings of the *Gītā*, (d) the *Āgamaprāmāṇya* which establishes the authority of the Pāñcarātra texts, (e) the *Siddhitraya* (*Ātma-, Īśvara-, Saṁvit- siddhis*) which depicts the nature of the individual soul and the superme Lord and their relation to each other. In the last section he refutes the doctrine of *avidyā* and Monism of Śaṅkara, (f) the *Mahāpuruṣanirṇaya* which is not available now, establishes the supremacy of Lord Nārāyaṇa over other deities on the strength of various authoritative texts (mainly the *Upaniṣads*) reinforced by reasonings.[^25]

It was given to Yāmuna only to lay down the fundamentals of Viśiṣṭādvaita philosophy and not to compose explanatory treatise (*bhāṣya*) on the *Vedāntasūtras* as Śaṅkara had done for Advaita. That task was achieved by Rāmānuja, said to have been named so by his maternal uncle who expected him to equal the great Lakṣmaṇa in his devotion to the Lord. Rāmānuja's father was a disciple of Śrīśailapūrṇa, his brother-in-law, Yāmuna's grandson and disciple who is said to have obtained from the Lord Himself the title of Tātācārya.[^26] The traditional date of Rāmānuja's birth is 1017 A.D. and he is

[^25]: M. Narasimhachary, *Contribution of Yāmuna to Viśiṣṭādvaita*, pp. 13-15.
[^26]: Vide V. Rangacharya, op.cit. p. 174.
[]
said to have lived for 120 years. Yāmuna died before Rāmānuja became an Ācārya, and the interval was filled up by two scholars, Mahāpūrṇa and Śrīśailapūrṇa.

Rāmānuja inherited three great tasks from his *guru*, Yāmuna - (i) the perpetuation of the memory of Parāśara (the author of the *Viṣṇupurāṇa*), (ii) the immortalisation of the glory of Nammāḻvār and (iii) the interpretation of Vyāsa's *Brahmasūtras* according to the Viśiṣṭādvaita system. During his long life-span Rāmānuja more than carried out these injunctions. He learnt Vedānta from Tirukkoṭṭiyūr Nambi, *Tiruvāymoḻi* from Tiruvaraṅga Perumāḷ Araiyar (Āḷavandār's son) and Tirumālai Āṇḍān; and *Rāmāyaṇa* from Periya Tirumalai Nambi. He is further said to have gone as far as Kashmir to see the *Vṛtti* or commentary of Bodhāyana which, according to tradition, interpreted the *Brahmasūtras* in the Viśiṣṭādvaita mode. These preparations being over, Rāmānuja gave the world in succession, several works which made him the foremost among the Śrīvaiṣṇava philosophers. One of his early works was the *Vedārthasaṅgraha* which deals in an elaborate manner with the teachings of the *Upaniṣads*. Rāmānuja attacks the Advaita interpretation of the *mahāvākya* "*Tattvamasi*", Śaṅkara's *Māyāvāda* and the Bhedābheda doctrines of Bhāskara and Yādavaprakāśa. He then reconciles the Vedic passages with one another and formulates Viśiṣṭādvaita. In the concluding section he proves beyond doubt that the supreme Deity is to be called Nārāyaṇa and not by any other name known to the *Upaniṣads*. His *Vedāntadīpa* and *Vedāntasāra* are
[]
comparatively shorter commentaries on the *Brahmasūtras*. His *Gadyatraya* comprising *Śaraṇāgati-*, *Śrīraṅga-* and *Vaikuṇṭha-* gadyas explains the concept of whole-hearted surrender called *śaraṇāgati* in this system, the glory of Lord Raṅganātha of Śrīraṅgam and the highest abode of the Lord (*Śrīvaikuṇṭha*). In his *Nityagrantha*, Rāmānuja explains the daily routine to be observed by a Śrīvaiṣṇava. His *Gītābhāṣya*, as the name suggests, is a commentary on the *Bhagavad-gītā*. He has bedecked the literary-cum-philosophical world with his Navaratna of which his magnum opus the *Śrībhāṣya* is the brightest gem. The Viśiṣṭādvaita philosophy expounded by Rāmānuja in his *Śrībhāṣya* appealed to minds which were not satisfied with the Advaitic teachings of Śaṅkara. While Śaṅkara appealed to the head through his idealism and doctrine of *Māyāvāda*, Rāmānuja appealed to the heart through his realism and doctrine of loving devotion (*bhakti*) and self-surrender (*prapatti*). After completing his *Śrībhāṣya* Rāmānuja, toured extensively throughout the country to popularise his teachings.

Rāmānuja is said to have fulfilled his second vow of perpetuating Parāśara's name, by calling upon Bhaṭṭa, a son of his friend and follower Kūreśa, who was also called Parāśara, to compose a commentary on the *Viṣṇusahasranāma*. This commentary, the *Bhagavadguṇadarpaṇa* (literally, the mirror reflecting the Lord's auspicious qualities) is indeed the Viśiṣṭādvaita commentary on the *Viṣṇusahasranāma*, extolling the Lord's
[]
*agaṇitakalyāṇaguṇas* as opposed to the Advaitic commentary of Śaṅkara stressing on the *paratva* of the Nirguṇa Brahman.

By authorising Kurukeśa, son of his uncle Śrīśailapūrṇa, to compose an authoritative commentary on the *Tiruvāymoḻi* as taught and expounded by him, Rāmānuja fulfilled his third vow of perpetuating Nammāḻvār's name. This commentary is known as the "Six thousand ", and is the earliest one on the hymns of Nammāḻvār. It was the model upon which many compositions were written later, particularly in the *maṇipravāḷa* style.

Rāmānuja was not only a profound thinker, he was also a great organiser. His reform of the Śrīraṅgam temple administration is a significant landmark in the development of systematic worship in the Śrīvaiṣṇavite temples. He divided the Śrīvaiṣṇava world into a number of *ācārya*-dioceses, over each of which he appointed a pious householder as the head or *siṁhāsanādhipati*. A set of seventy-four spiritual leaders (*ācāryapuruṣas*) took up the work of teaching Viśiṣṭādvaita in the towns and villages of South India.

This move by Rāmānuja was an intermediate stage of informal organisations between the earlier system of personal choice of teacher-disciple that prevailed during the Nāthamuni - Rāmānuja period (9th or 10th century-early 12th century) and the period of the establishment of formal Maṭhas (14th
[]
century).[^27] It is not possible to trace in detail the entire succession in regard to these 74 *ācāryas*. Some families, however, still bear the names of these *ācāryas* like Embār, Nallān Cakravarti, Āḷkoṇḍa Villi and so on.

It was but natural that when these *ācāryas* started teaching Viśiṣṭādvaita in tune with Rāmānuja's viewpoint, slightly varied interpretations according to individual perferences for Sanskrit and Tamil languages began to crop up. In course of time, these differences became marked (i.e. not merely *abhiprāya* and *ācārabheda*, but differences in ideas of *prapatti*, divine grace and position of Śrī) and gradually there emerged two major divisions in the Śrīvaiṣṇava community-the Northern school or Vaḍakalai and the Southern school or Teṅgalai. Here North and South are not in any way indicative of North and South India. Vaḍakalai indicates the school of thought prevalent in the northern part of the Tamil country with its cultural capital at Kāñcīpuram and Teṅgalai indicates the school of thought prevalent in the southern part of the Tamil country with its cultural capital at Śrīraṅgam. The usual and popular view is that the Vaḍakalai sect became distinct with Vedāntadeśika as its *ācārya* and the Teṅgalai sect became distinct with Maṇavāḷa Māmuṉi as its principal *ācārya*.

[^27]: N. Jagadeesan, *History of Śrī Vaiṣṇavism in the Tamil Country (Post-Rāmānuja)*, p. 47, Koodal Publishers, Madurai, 1977.
[]
It should be pointed out in the true spirit of Śrīvaiṣṇavism that this emergence of two principal sects was only a natural evolution. A true Śrīvaiṣṇavite instead of condemning the rift and the resulting tension between the two sects, should appreciate the positive aspect of this division. To the true devotee of Śrīvaiṣṇavism deeply rooted in South India, the Vaḍakalai sect constitutes the secondary root spreading all over the northern part of Tamil country and the Teṅgalai sect constitutes the secondary root spreading across the southern part of the Tamil country viz. the Kāveri delta centering around Śrīraṅgam. To a student of Śrīvaiṣṇavism this diversification, without secession from the mainstream of Śrīvaiṣṇavism, provides a treasure-house of invaluable literature which otherwise would never have been penned at all. It stands testimony to the "unity in diversity" principle incorporated in Śrīvaiṣṇavism. It is moreover indicative of the *sūkṣmabuddhi* (subtle intellect) of *pūrvācāryas* like Yāmuna and Rāmānuja whose philosophical works were capable of variegated interpretations, intelligent and thought-provoking in their own ways. It goes to the credit of later *ācāryas* like Vedāntadeśika and Maṇavāḷa Māmuṉi to have enriched the Śrīvaiṣṇava literary world with their innumerable commentaries. A true Śrīvaiṣṇavite, hence will not bemoan the split, if one may call it, but will thank his *ācāryas* for having provided valuable food for his thought.
[]
The standard differences between the Vaḍakalais and the Teṅgalais are usually enumerated as eighteen and are termed *aṣṭādaśa bhedas*. One of the main points of dispute is the importance which each sect attaches to Sanskrit and Tamil languages. While the Vaḍakalais give priority to the chanting of *Vedas* in temples, the Teṅgalais give preference to the recitation of Tamil *prabandhams*. *Prapatti* is a philosophical point which divides Vaḍakalais and Teṅgalais. The word "*prapatti*" means "an approach to God". For the Vaḍakalais "*prapatti*" is qualified by certain conditions: a) acquisition of qualities which would make one fit for offering to God; b) avoidance of conduct not acceptable to God; c) faith that God alone is the protector; d) appeal for protection; e) a feeling of helplessness; and f) absolute surrender.

`आनुकूल्यस्य सङ्कल्पः प्रातिकूल्यस्य वर्जनम्।`
`रक्षिष्यतीति विश्वासो गोप्तृत्ववरणं तथा।`
`आत्मनिक्षेप-कार्पण्ये षड्विधा शरणागतिः॥`[^28]

In this *aṅga-aṅgi-samuccaya*, *ātma-nikṣepa* alone is the *aṅgin* and the rest are *aṅgas*. This is proved beyond doubt by the following verse:

`निक्षेपापरपर्यायो न्यासः पञ्चाङ्ग-संयुतः।`
`सन्न्यासः त्याग इत्युक्तः शरणागतिरित्यपि॥`[^29]

For the Teṅgalais this *prapatti* is not qualified by any conditions. It is the only way for salvation. It is the first as well as the last way, and it can be

[^28]: *Ahirbudhnya Saṁhitā*, 37- 28,29.
[^29]: *Lakṣmī Tantra*, Adyar Library Series, 17-74.
[]
performed even in the beginning i.e. without attempting other ways to salvation like *jñāna* and *bhakti yogas*. The Vaḍakalai point of view is that *prapatti* is to be attempted only as a last resort, after failing in *karma, jñāna* and *bhakti yogas*. These differences in turn leads to the question of whether Divine grace is set in motion by human effort or not i.e. whether it is *sahetuka* or *nirhetuka*. This again fringes on the question as to whether man has to expiate for his sins and then obtain Divine Grace, or whether Divine Grace being irresistible, compassion and grace are showered on erring souls, ignoring their faults or even delighting in their sins because it provides God with an opportunity for showering His Grace. This situation is somewhat eased by introducing "*Śrī*" as the *puruṣakāra* or Mediator, to intervene for mercy on behalf of erring souls to the Ever just and Lawful Lord. Her role as Divine Mother is accomplished by Her essential characteristic of compassion and for the Vaḍakalais Śrī is *puruṣakāra*; She is the *upāya* (means) even like Śrīman Nārāyaṇa for attaining salvation and She is also *upeya* (goal) like Lord Nārāyaṇa in Vaikuṇṭha attended upon by ever-liberated souls (*nityasūris*) and other liberated souls. She is the *paramātmā* like Nārāyaṇa and is not *jīvātmā*. She is also *vibhu* (all-pervasive) like Nārāyaṇa and can grant liberation (*mokṣa*). The Teṅgalais accept Her role as mediator and agree that She is a means for liberation. But they disagree in that She is a *jīvātmā* and is finite (*aṇu*) in nature.
[]
There is another point of difference regarding *kaivalya* which consists in self-realisation. While the Teṅgalais consider those who have reached *kaivalya* as liberated, the Vaḍagalais maintain that *kaivalya* is inferior to *paramapada* and is situated outside Vaikuṇṭha. There are also differences regarding the interpretation of the *caramaśloka* (*Gītā*, xviii, 66). The phrase "*sarvadharmān parityajya*" is interpreted by Vaḍakalais to mean those who are in capable of performing *bhakti yoga* but the Teṅgalais interpret it as "having to give up all duties" before seeking refuge in the Lord. Post-*prapatti* sins are to be atoned for afresh by Vaḍakalais but for the Teṅgalais, *prapatti* once done holds good forever. One other point of difference relates to the performance of one's daily duties (*nityakarma*) like *sandhyāvandana* etc.; for the Teṅgalais a *prapanna* need not perform these duties.

In conclusion it may be said that these differences were a natural result of the evolution in religious thought in the course of centuries. Neither the Āḻvārs nor the early Ācāryas spelt them out. In course of time this gave rise to subdivisions within Vaḍakalais, like the followers of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha, Śrī Āṇḍavan Āśrama, Parakāla Maṭha and within Teṅgalais like Vānamāmalai Maṭha and Yatirāja Maṭha. These divisions are not exhaustive, because numerous groups based on differences of *deśācāra* (local variations) tend to develop. The differences generally arise out of local traditions, affiliations to particular Ācāryas and Maṭhas and family practices. However it should be
[]
noted that the *Pāñcarātra* and *Vaikhānasa Āgamas* are not associated with Vaḍakalai and Teṅgalai sects, but cut across this division.

The following is the Vaḍakalai succession table originating from Lord Nārāyaṇa upto Ādi Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa Jīyar, the founder of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha (1378 A.D.). It is not a family tree but rather *guru-śiṣya* lineage.[^30]

[^30]: See *Śrī Nṛsiṁha Priyā* (Tamil), April 1966.
[]

*   Perumāḷ (Viṣṇu)
*   Pirāṭṭi (Śrī)
*   Senainātha (Viṣvaksena)
*   Nammāḻvār (Śaṭhakopa)
*   Nāthamuni
*   Uyyakkoṇḍār (Puṇḍarīkākṣa)

*   Āḷavandār (Yāmuna)
  *   Periya Nambi (Mahāpūrṇa) (Brahma Sūtras, Gītā and Rahasyārthas)
  *   Tirumālai Āṇḍāṉ (Bhagavad Viṣayam)
    *   Rāmānuja (Śrībhāṣyakāra)

*   Egaḷ Āḻvāṉ

*   Keśavācārya
*   **Śrīmad Ādivaṇ Śaṭhakopa Yatīndra Mahādeśika** (Founder Jīyar of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha)

[]
### Śrī Ahobilam

#### The Lord and the Kṣetra

`उग्रोत्त्युग्र स्वरूप स्वभक्तजनां नुकेसरी।`
`केसरीव स्वपोतानां अन्वेष्टा उग्रविग्रहः॥`

The unique Man-Lion, Lord Narasiṁha has incarnated Himself in *arcā* form in several places. The most important among them is Śrī Ahobilam because according to our ancient scriptures (the *Brahmāṇḍapurāṇa*) this is the place where Narasiṁha *avatāra* and *Hiraṇya-saṁhāra* took place. The legend of the *avatāra* of Lord Narasiṁha is also told in the *Bhāgavata-*, the *Vāyu-*, the *Padma-* and the *Kūrma- purāṇas*, and in the *Harivaṁśa*. The *Taittirīya Āraṇyaka* contains the earliest allusion to Narasiṁha. The *Nṛsiṁhapurāṇa* begins with a salutation to Lord Narasiṁha and narrates the legend of Narasiṁha through sage Vyāsa's disciple Romaharṣaṇa.

The presiding deity of Śrī Ahobilam is variously called – Narasiṁha, Nṛsiṁha, Ahobileśvara, Ahobila Narasiṁha, Ahobila Nātha. He is said to have emerged out of a pillar in Hiraṇyakaśipu's palace on the fourteenth day of the bright fortnight of the Vaiśākha month (*vaiśākhaśukla-caturdaśī*). In Indian tradition that day is regarded as Narasiṁha Jayantī.[^31] The Lord is described in the *Nṛsiṁhasahasranāmastotra* as *para-dharma, para-jñāna*, and

[^31]: Śrīvaiṣṇavas celebrate only three Jayantīs of the Lord - those of Śrī Rāma, Śrī Kṛṣṇa and Śrī Narasiṁha.
[]
*para-bhakti*. He is a form of Lord Nārāyaṇa and is the source of all mantras. He is full of effulgence and brilliance (*tejomaya*). He is known for His roaring (*nāda*) and big chortle (*aṭṭahāsa*).

The Lord in this *avatāra* is said to have lived in Śrī Ahobilam after killing Hiraṇyakaśipu. Traditional accounts relate that He seemed to have completely forgotten about Mahālakṣmī and His original abode, Śrī Vaikuṇṭha. Since Goddess Śrī cannot remain without Her Lord, She devised a plan to join Him. The forest tribes who lived in and around Śrī Ahobilam are called Ceñcus. Goddesss Śrī took birth as the daughter of a Ceñcu chief. Enamoured by Her ravishing beauty, the Lord married Her and thus Lord Nārāyaṇa and the Universal Mother were united once more. Hence it is, that the consort of the Lord here is called Ceñcu Lakṣmī. As a result, the Ceñcu tribes became ardent votaries of Lord Viṣṇu.

#### Location

Ahobilam is one of the three sacred places in the long range of disjointed hills that go to make up that stretch of Eastern Ghats from Krishna river down south to the Tirupati Hills - called "Nalla Malais". This range, nearly 250 miles long, starts from the sacred Śaivite temple Mallikārjuna - called "Śrīśailaparvatam" situated in the northern end of the river Krishna. The middle shrine is indeed Śrī Ahobilam, a Śrīvaiṣṇavite shrine of great
[]
antiquity. Tirupati or Tirumala is, of course, the Śrīvaiṣṇavite shrine of Lord Śrī Veṅkaṭeśvara at the southern end.

**Ahobalam** (Lo! what strength!) or **Ahobilam** (Lo! what a cave!) is a village and a shrine in the Nalla Malais (Black Mountain or in Tamil "Good Mountain") in the Siruvel Taluk of Kurnool District between 15° 8' N and 78° 45' E. It has two parts namely **Diguva** (Lower) Ahobilam Temple at the foot of the hills, **Eguva** (Upper) or **Pedda** Ahobilam about four miles higher up, and a small shrine on the summit. While the Eguva temple is considered to be more important from the religious view-point, the Lower temple is architecturally more interesting. It contains beautiful panels from the *Rāmāyaṇa* inscribed on its walls. It has two great stone *maṇṭapams* supported by pillars, eight feet in circumference, hewn out of rock. Fergusson mentions that a Kalyāṇa Maṇṭapam (wedding hall) supported by 64 pillars (each of which is carved into several miniature pillars) is a fine specimen of architecture and though wanting in delicacy and elegance, is full of character and merit.[^32]

This place has been sung by the Śrīvaiṣṇava saint, Tirumaṅgai Āḻvār in his mystic outpourings. It is one of the 108 *Divya Deśas* praised by the

[^32]: 'Dravidian Style', *History of India and Eastern Architecture*, I, iv, 404, London John Murray Albermale Dt. W. 1910.
[]
Āḻvārs. He has celebrated this deity in a decad called 'Aṅgaṇ ñālam'[^33] : "The elephants at the forest-shrine of Ahobilam pay obeisance to Lord Narasiṁha by offering their ivory tusks." This hymn is interpreted by the pontiffs of Ahobila Maṭha as a symbolic reference to the future Ācāryas (acting as mediators) who will accept the surrender of their devotees and offer them to the Lord.

Ādi Śaṅkara praises this deity in his two poems, *Śrī Lakṣmīnṛsiṁha-karāvalamba-stotra* and *Śrī Lakṣmīnṛsiṁhapañcaratnastotram*. The *Guru-paramparāprabhāvam* speaks of Nammāḻvār's visit to this place in search of Lord Narasiṁha:

ஆடியாடி யகம் கரைந்து, இசை
பாடிப்பரவிக் கண்ணீர் மல்கி, எங்கும்
நாடிநாடி நரசிங்கா வென்று
வாடிவாடு மிவ்வா னுதலே.[^34]

Rāmānuja's visit is also found mentioned in the *Guruparamparāprabhāvam Āṟāyirappaṭi*, as also that of Vedāntadeśika and Nayinārācārya. Rāmānuja is said to have issued an edict that Narasiṁha worship should be done daily by all Siṁhāsanādhipatis. Vādikesari Aḻagiya

[^33]: அங்கண் ஞாலம் ... பைங்கண் ஆனைக்கொம்பு கொண்டு பத்திமையால், அடிக்கீழ்ச் செங்கண் ஆளி யிட்டு இறைஞ்சும் சிங்கவேள் குன்றமே. - *Periya-tirumoḻi*, 1-7-1.
[^34]: *Tiruvāymoḻi*, 2-4-1.
[]
Maṇavāḷa Jīyar (author of *Śrī Nṛsiṁha Aṣṭakastotram*) and Divyakavi Piḷḷai Perumāḷ Ayyaṅgār[^35] have also sung in praise of the Lord. The *Caitanya Vijaya* speaks of the Bengali saint Śrī Caitanya's visit to Ahobilam.

Tradition has it that it is the idol form of the Lord that was worshipped by the *Vibhava* form of the same Lord, in Śrī Rāma Avatāra and Lord Śrī Veṅkaṭeśvara (Lord of Seven Hills). It is said that Lord Śrī Rāma worshipped Lord Narasiṁha in this place and sang five verses in His praise. It is known as *Narasiṁha Pañcāmṛtam* found in the *Śeṣa Dharma*, 47th chapter of the *Harivaṁśa*. It is said that only after such a worship did Lord Śrī Rāma get information about Śrī Sītā's whereabouts which ultimately led to Rāvaṇavadha and reunion with Sītā Devī. In the *Veṅkaṭācalamāhātmya* it is mentioned that Lord Śrīnivāsa and Goddess Padmāvatī worshipped the Lord at Ahobilam and were then united in divine wedlock. Hence, like Śrīraṅganātha, Lord Lakṣmī Nṛsiṁha is also called "Periya Perumāḷ" - the great God or God of gods.

The Holy shrine of Ahobilam is termed as *Navanṛsiṁha-kṣetram* as nine forms of Lord Narasiṁha are housed in these forest shrines.

[^35]: *Nūttreṭṭu Tirupati Antādi, Aṣṭa-prabandham*, p. 295.
[]
ज्वालाहोबिलमालोलकोडकारञ्जिभार्गवाः।
योगानन्दच्छत्रवट-पावना नवमूर्तयः॥[^36]

The hills of Ahobilam or Siṅgavelkuṉṟam as the Āḻvārs extolled this place, symbolise the trunk of Ādi-śeṣa, Lord Viṣṇu's serpent, its hood being Tirumala and its tail Śrīśaila.

Of the nine Narasiṁha shrines, the Mālola Narasiṁha is the Ārādhyadevatā of the Jīyars of Ahobilam. This idol is said to have been installed by Sage Bharadvāja. It is significant in that it is the *śāntarūpa* of the Lion-God. We have the Lokamātā Mahālakṣmī seated on the lap of Her pacified Lord - the Lord who delights or pleases the Universal Mother. The Lord is *Lakṣmyā samāliṅgitavāmabhāga, for bhaktānugraha*.

#### The Greatness of Narasiṁha Avatāra

It is a well-known fact that the *vibhava* form (*avatāra*) of Śrīman Nārāyaṇa descending from Śrīvaikuṇṭha to this mundane world is for *duṣṭanigraha* and *śiṣṭaparipālana*. In the Lord's mind, the *śiṣṭaparipālana* is given pride of place by the Lord's own words in the *Bhagavad Gītā* (IV. 8):

`परित्राणाय साधूनां विनाशाय च दुष्कृताम्।`

[^36]: See *Śrī Nṛsiṁha Priyā* (English) Twelfth Annual Number, Special Issue, Vol. 13, p. 10, June 1992, Hyderabad.
[]
The use of the particle *ca* is significant in that, during the process of affording protection to His devotees, the destruction of evil forces ensues as a natural result. It is this noble trait of *śiṣṭaparipālana* that lends an added charm to the glory of Lord's *avatāra* as "Narasiṁha" –

`नरवत् सिंहवत् चैव रूपं यस्य महात्मनः।`[^37]

the beatific and beautiful Man-Lion form. It incorporates the "unity in diversity" principle very aptly. Here the two apparently diverse elements - the *ugra* and the *bhadra nṛsiṁhas* are interfused into this unique form.

Let us consider the circumstances leading to this glorious *avatāra*. We have the *akiñcana-bhakta-dānavaśiśu* Prahlāda unarmed, standing before the terrifying demon Hiraṇyakaśipu, and chanting with immense and intense faith, *harināma* and *harināma* alone, though taunted and tormented by his own father to recite his name. Undaunted the child stood, spurning all material wealth and pleasures, ready to face innumerable travails for the sake of his Lord Hari and declaring in unequivocal terms that Lord Hari alone is omnipotent, omnipresent and omniscient, and that no power in the world can disprove it. It is no wonder that the Lord (becomes a slave as it were, to such a devotee who has *mahāviśvāsa*) rushed to his succour unasked for, and impregnated all things great and small, *tṛṇa* and *stambha* alike with the

[^37]: *Mantrarājapadastotra*, 6.
[]
unique form of Narasiṁha. Unable to brook *hiṁsā* in any form (thought, word or deed) to such a Bhakta, and to prove the child's assertion of His omnipresence, the Lord manifested Himself as Narasiṁha from that particular pillar which the Dānava king pin-pointed and struck with his palm. It is added as a beautiful after-thought by Vedāntadeśika that except for that one pillar in Hiraṇyakaśipu's palace, all the three worlds are still impregnated with the form of Narasiṁha:

`स्तम्भस्य दानवशिशोः परिपालनाय भद्रां नृसिंहघटनामधिजागृषस्ते।`
`स्तम्भैकवर्जमधुनपि करीश! नूनं त्रैलोक्यमेतदखिलं नरसिंहगर्भम्॥`[^38]

One other aspect of this *avatāra* which Vedāntadeśika highlights is that the Lord removed the odium of childlessness even in the case of an inanimate object the *stambha*; and in this process, enabled it to become a grandmother of Brahmā.

`या काचित् सहसा महासुरगृहस्थूणा पितामह्यभूत्।`[^39]

This *avatāra* exhibits a unique combination of the Lord's *paratva* and *saulabhya* - the *para* aspect of the Lord (delineating the *ugrasiṁha* aspect),

`सटापटलभीषणे सरभसाट्टहासोद्भटे`
`स्फुरत्क्रुधि परिस्फुटद्भृकुटिकेडऽपि वक्त्रे कृते।`[^40]

[^38]: *Varadarājapañcāśat*, 23.
[^39]: *Daśāvatārastotra*, 5.
[^40]: *Kāmāsikāṣṭaka*, 7.
[]
showing the fury and anger of the Lord when His helpless *paramabhakta* is tortured and the *saulabhya* aspect of the same Lord delineating the *vātsalya* and *karuṇā* of the human form (*kapaṭakesarin*)

`कृपा कपटकेसरिन् दनुजडिम्भदत्तस्तना`
`सरोजसदृशा दृशा व्यतिविषणज्य ते व्यज्यते॥`[^41]

showing the motherly affection of the same Lord towards the youngest and perhaps His greatest devotee Prahlāda, suckling him with the "milk of human kindness". The Divine grace and fury functioning in the same *avatāra* simultaneously and in the same organ (the Eyes) has been pointed out beautifully by none other than *kavisiṁha* Vedāntadeśika who highlights the Lord's *aghaṭita-ghaṭanā-sāmarthya* (capacity to bring together the incompatible and the impossible). Vedāntadeśika uses almost similar (same) words in his *Dayāśatakam* (84) also:

`दये वृषगिरीशितुर्दनुजडिम्भदत्तस्तना`
`सरोजसदृशा दृशा समुदिताकृतिर्द्दश्यसे॥`

Śrīvatsāṅkamiśra (Kūratāḻvāṉ) has extolled this unique Man-Lion *avatāra*. Born out of a pillar, neither man nor beast and yet both man and lion—His very *avatāra* bespeaks His supremacy and transcendance. These extraordinary features certainly do give away His *paratva*.

`नरसिंहतनुर्गुणी समसमयसमुद्भवश्च भक्तगिरः।`
`स्तम्भे च सम्भवस्ते पिशुनयति परेषतां वरद!॥`[^42]

[^41]: *Kāmāsikāṣṭaka*, 7.
[^42]: *Varadarājastava*, 71.
[]
Parāśarabhaṭṭa, in his commentary on the *Viṣṇusahasranāma* - the *Bhagavad-guṇadarpaṇa* explains the word *Nārasiṁhavapuḥ*[^43] thus:

`स्वभक्त्यन्तरायनिवारणाय प्रदर्शिता।`
`तत् क्षणप्रणतिपत्र यथाकामदिदृक्षया महानृसिंहसंहननः`

and adds that He is *Śrīmān*[^44] -

`परस्पर-दुर्धर-अपूर्व-विकृतस्वरूप-परिग्रहादप्रिश्रीमान् -`
`शौन्दर्यलावण्यादिभिः अतिमनोहरादिरूपः।`

Tirumaḻiśai Āḻvār (Bhaktisāra) expresses this idea in a similar strain[^45] - "because He assumes the form of the Lion, He is beautiful." One cannot look for a better credential for the charm and beauty of Lord Narasiṁha than what has been said by Godā (Āṇḍāḷ), when she dreams about her wedding and relates that dream to her friend and refers to the form of Narasiṁha twice viz. (i) when she enters the marriage hall[^46] and (ii) when He holds Her hands above His, during the *lāja-homa*.[^47] He was "*Kāle Nṛsiṁha*"[^48] to Rukmiṇī Devī, in her love-epistle to Lord Kṛṣṇa. This *avatāra* stands as a class apart in that it was

[^43]: p.148 *Viśiṣṭādvaita Pracāriṇi Sabha*, 1983.
[^44]: Ibid. p. 149.
[^45]: *Iyarpa* - *Nānmukan Tiruvantādi*, 22: அழகியான் தானே அறியுருவன் தானே
[^46]: *Nācciyār Tirumoḻi*, vi, 2: கோளரி மாதவன் கோவிந்தன்...
[^47]: Ibid.vi, 9: அரிமுகன் அச்சுதன் கைம்மேல் என் கைவைத்து பொரிமுகந் தட்டக் கனாக் கண்டேன் தோழீ நான்.
[^48]: *Bhāgavatapurāṇa*, X, lii, 38.
[]
instantaneous, at the right moment and the achievement of its purpose too was instantaneous.

Another fact that surfaces from this glorious *avatāra* is that the Lord's compassion, all-encompassing as it is, extends even to the demon king Hiraṇyakaśipu who hated Him most. Obsessed by thoughts of Lord Hari, His Greatness and Immanence, he uttered the Lord's Name with hatred and fear. Ironically this earned liberation for him in the end.

It will be fitting to conclude this chapter with the following second *śloka* of the *Ārtatrāṇa-parāyaṇa Nārāyaṇastotra*:[^49]

`प्रह्लादार्ति यदीश्वरो व्रद हरिः सर्वत्र मे दर्शय`
`स्तम्भे चैनमिति ब्रुवन्तमसुरं तत्राविरासीद्धरिः।`
`वक्षस्तस्य विदारयन् निजजने वात्सल्यमावेदयन्`
`आर्तत्राणपरायणः स भगवान् नारायणो मे गतिः॥`

[^49]: Ascribed to Kūreśa.
[]
## SAṀNYĀSA DHARMA IN ŚRĪVAIṢṆAVISM

The contribution of holy persons and *saṁnyāsins* (termed variously as sages, saints, ascetics) to the development of world religions and thought is invaluable. The Indian panorama, fused as it is with variegated cultures and religions provides an unending line of these enlightened souls (Śaṅkara, Rāmānuja, Madhva, Mahāvīra, Buddha and so on) who spurned material pleasures and worked for the service of humanity. It is to these ascetics that India owes largely all that is valuable and enduring in her cultural and spiritual life.

The *saṁnyāsa* tradition or asceticism is not new to India. S. Radhakrishnan observes that from the beginning of her history, India has adored and idolised not soldiers and statesmen, not men of science and industry, not even poets and philosophers, but those rare and chastened spirits – the saints or ascetics. The spectacle of these holy souls walking from one corner of the country to the other through dust and din, in rain and sunshine, unmindful of the privations and ever seeking the goal of the divine and spreading the message of the divine is a familiar sight from days of yore. The concept of asceticism has so much fascinated the western mind that the French philosopher Renou in his *Religions of Ancient India* considers Hinduism as the very type of a religion of renunciation.[^50] J.C. Oman in *The Mystics, Ascetics*

[^50]: *Hinduism*, p. 5. University of London, Althone Press, London, 1953.
[]
*and Saints of India* goes to the extent of declaring that the only possible state of religious life is the one involving asceticism and feels that the very spirit of the East is found to be embodied in the *ascetic dharma*.[^51]

According to the *Smṛtis*, a *dvija* (twice-born) should pass through the four stages of life:of a student (Brahmacārin), a householder (Gṛhastha), a hermit (Vānaprastha) and finally an ascetic (Saṁnyāsin). In days of yore, the fourth stage was permitted only to old people after a well-spent life.[^52] Later, a Brahmacārin was admitted into the order, if his tongue, emotions, appetite and speech are pure and under strict control.[^53] The *Jābālopaniṣad* allows the option to regard *saṁnyāsa* as the fourth stage after any of the first two *āśramas*. Owing to the great similarity between the third and fourth stage, the third stage came to be gradually ignored and people passed directly from the life of a householder to that of an ascetic.

In the Śrīvaiṣṇava tradition, this *āśrama* is considered as a very exalted stage of one's life and is assigned to the final stages of one's existence. This system has been sanctified by Yāmunācārya, Rāmānuja, Embār, Nañjīyar, Periya Jīyar, Brahmatantrasvatantra Jīyar, Ādi Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa Jīyar, Rāmānuja

[^51]: T. Fisher Unwin, Paternoster Square, London, 1905, p.10.
[^52]: A. Barth, *Religions of India*, p. 79, Sixth Edition, S. Chand & Co, Delhi, 1969.
[^53]: Vide *Nṛsiṁha Purāṇa*, 60-2-4.
[]
Jīyar, Śrīmad Āṇḍavan Svāmī and so on. It is said that there were over 700 *saṁnyāsins* (ascetics) in Rāmānuja's entourage. Rāmānuja was so zealous to uphold the ideals of this *āśrama* that he commissioned his (former Advaita preceptor) disciple Yādava Prakāśa to write out a treatise on *saṁnyāsa dharma* entitled the *Yatidharmasamuccaya*. As there are exceptions to every rule, in this glittering line of Ācāryas (preceptors), we have the examples of Nāthamuni and Vedānta Deśika, who have accomplished all this and more, without entering into the fourth *āśrama*.

The ascetic is a symbol of holy life. One who enters the superior order of an ascetic not only separates himself from all temporal ties and carnal affections, but also rises superior to the householder (*gṛhastha*) who conforms to the rites and ceremonies enjoined by ordinary *karmakāṇḍa*. Sage Yājñavalkya states that the difference between a *saṁnyāsin* and a *gṛhastha* is comparable to the difference between the resplendent sun and the firefly; the difference between the huge Mt. Meru and the minute mustard seed.

`सूर्यर्षभयोर्यद्वदन्तरं मेरुसर्षपयोर्यथा।`
`अन्तरं हि महद् दृष्टं तथा भिक्षुगृहस्थयोः॥`[^54]

He is a *saṁnyāsin* because he is a *sarvasaṅga parityāgī* i.e. he abandons all earthly possessions – father, mother, wife, son and daughter. It is a total secesssion from domestic life followed by certain purificatory

[^54]: See *Śrī Nṛsiṁha Priyā*, p. 4, August 1992.
[]
ceremonies as laid down in the sacred texts. Thereafter he becomes a *bhikṣu* (beggar) living purely on alms, a *parivrājaka* (itinerant) wandering around, homeless. He is given to severe austerities and self-mortification and is concerned principally with restraint of senses and contemplation of the highest reality, Brahman. Manu declares, "Let the unimpassioned man relinquish all affections for wife, children and possessions and enter the fourth order. Let him forego the three objects of human existence viz. pleasure, wealth and virtue."[^55] In the *Nāradaparivrājakopaniṣad*[^56] the characteristics of an ascetic are laid down as follows:

`कामः क्रोधस्तथा दर्पो लोभमोहादयश्च ये।`
`तांस्तु दोषान् परित्यज्य परिपूर्णमयो भवेत्॥`

`रागद्वेषवियुक्तात्मा समलोष्टाश्मकाञ्चनः।`
`प्राणहिंसानिवृत्तश्च मुनिः स्यात्सर्वान्निरस्पृहः॥`

`दम्भाहङ्कारनिर्मुक्तो हिंसापैशून्यवर्जितः।`
`आत्मज्ञानगुणोपेतो यतिर्मोक्षमवाप्नुयात्॥`

Sage Jamadagni declares that even the external guise of a *saṁnyāsin* has its own greatness[^57] –

`दुर्बलो वा सुवृत्तो वा मूर्खः पण्डित एव वा।`
`काषायमात्रवेषेण (दण्डमात्रेण) यतिः पूज्यो युधिष्ठिर॥`

[^55]: VI - 33.
[^56]: III - 33, 35, 59.
[^57]: Yādavaprakāśa, *Yatidharmasamuccaya*, with Appendix, *Yatidharma-saṅgraha*, p. 85, Pub. Śrī Veṅkaṭeśa Granthamālā, Śrīmad Ubhaya Vedāntācārya Pīṭham, Śrīraṅgam, 1973.
[]
The Lord Himself has declared in the *Śrāddhaprakaraṇa* of the *Mahābhārata*[^58] thus –

`यत्र भिक्षुगृहं तत्र तं च मां विद्धि पाण्डव।`

"Wherever you see a *saṁnyāsin*, Oh Pāṇḍava! know him to be Myself."

Through this we come to know that the *saṁnyāsin* is of the nature of Lord Viṣṇu (*viṣṇusvarūpa*). The same point is brought out by Sage Dattātreya:[^59]

`त्रिदण्डिस्वरूपविप्रः साक्षान्नारायणात्मकः।`
`यस्तु पूजयते भक्त्या विष्णुस्तेन प्रपूजितः॥`
`द्वे रूपे वासुदेवस्य चलं चाचलमेव च।`
`चलं संन्यासिनां रूपम् अचलं प्रतिमादिकम्॥`

"A *tridaṇḍi saṁnyāsin* is none other than Lord Nārāyaṇa and one who worships a *saṁnyāsin* is actually worshipping Lord Viṣṇu Himself. Lord Vāsudeva has two forms - one moving and the other fixed. The moving form is *saṁnyāsin* and the fixed form is the idol form (*arcā rūpa*) as worshipped in temples."

The *Yatiliṅgasamarthana* of Vātsya Varadācārya is in three *skandhas* and presents the characteristics, qualifications and duties of the four classes of Śrīvaiṣṇava ascetics. It is based on the *Śāṭyānakopaniṣad* and *Smṛti* texts like

[^58]: *Yatidharmasamuccaya*, p. 84.
[^59]: Ibid, pp. 74-75.
[]
those of Hārīta, Atri, Vṛddhajābāli, Uśanas, Aṅgiras, Śaunaka and Śāṇḍilya.

Quoting Hārīta, Vātsya Varadācārya states:

`चातुर्विध्यं भिक्षवस्तु स्मृताः सामान्यलिङ्गिनः।`
`तेषां पृथक् पृथङ्नाम वृत्तिभेदात् कृतं श्रुतौ॥`
`कुटीचको बहुदकः हंसश्चैव तृतीयकः।`
`चतुर्थः परमो हंसो यो यः पश्चात् स उत्तमः॥`
`लिङ्गं तु वैष्णवं तेषां त्रिदण्डं स पवित्रकम्॥`[^60]

The Śrīvaiṣṇava ascetics fall into four groups namely Kuṭīcaka, Bahūdaka, Haṁsa and Paramahaṁsa, each latter one being superior to the previous one.

*Saṁnyāsa* is to be resorted to in the Uttarāyaṇa, when the sun is in its northward course. The intendant has to find an ascetic possessed of the virtue of quiescence. He should observe him and note for three months the duties of the ascetic. He should purify himself with the chanting (*japa*) of the sacred Gāyatrī and should perform a sacrifice to Prajāpati. According to the *Yajurveda*,[^61] he should be a *tridaṇḍin*. Hārīta[^62] states:

`त्रिदण्डं वैष्णवं सोम्यं सत्त्वञ्च समपर्वकम्।`
`वेष्टितं कृष्णगोबालरज्ज्वा तु चतुरङ्गुलम्॥`

[^60]: *Śāstra Muktāvali Series*, p. 3, ed. P.B. Ananthacharya, Sri Sudarsana Press, Conjeeveram, 1911.
[^61]: III-58.
[^62]: *Yatiliṅgasamarthana*, p. 6.
[]
An ascetic should take three staves of bamboo and tie them together with a rope of cow's tail, four *aṅgulas* in length.

A *tridaṇḍin* according to Manu[^63] is one who has restraint over his speech, mind and body, and does not imply mere physical appearance of carrying a *tridaṇḍa*.

`वाग्दण्डोऽथ मनोदण्डः कायदण्डस्तथैव च।`
`यस्यैते निहिता बुद्धौ त्रिदण्डीति स उच्यते॥`

A *saṁnyāsin* should always possess the following five things: *yajñopavīta, tridaṇḍa, jalapavitra, kaupīna* and *kaṭivastra*.[^64]

`उपवीतं त्रिदण्डं च पात्रं जलपवित्रकम्।`
`कौपीनं कटिवस्त्रं च न त्याज्यं यावदायुषम्॥`

The *tridaṇḍa* itself is *viṣṇurūpa* and has to be carried always.

`विष्णुरूपं त्रिदण्डाह्वयं सर्वथा धारयेद्यतिः।`

A *Pāñcarātra* text, *Tattvasāgarasaṁhitā*[^65] lays down thus –

`त्रिदण्डंस्थाय्याग्रभागं तु संस्मरेत् परमेष्ठिनम्।`
`द्वितीयं पुरुषमात्मानं विश्वात्मानं तु मध्यमम्॥`
`त्रिदण्डं समुदायमेवं विष्णुरूपं स्मरेद्यतिः।`

The Vyūhamūrtis viz. Vāsudeva, Saṅkarṣaṇa, Pradyumna and Aniruddha, and finally Lord Nārāyaṇa are integrated into the *tridaṇḍa*. These

[^63]: *Manusmṛti*, XII. 10.
[^64]: *Yatiliṅgasamarthana*, p. 8.
[^65]: *Yatidharmasamuccaya*, p. 16.
[]
five forms have Parameṣṭhyātmā, Puruṣātmā, Viśvātmā, Nivṛttyātmā and Sarvātmā respectively as their attributes. The *tridaṇḍa* should be mentally divided into five parts and should be meditated upon by the *saṁnyāsin* from the tip (*Parameṣṭyātmā*) to the trunk (*Sarvātmā*) respectively as the forms of Lord Viṣṇu. Hence, whenever the *tridaṇḍa* is placed down, a mantra with the following meaning is uttered: "Oh ground! may you become the sanctified place for the *tridaṇḍa* envisaging Lord Viṣṇu's form." Hence, whenever the *tridaṇḍa* is carried, the following mantra is to be uttered: "*Sakhā me gopāya!*", "friend, protect me!". Can a mere *acetana* bamboo *tridaṇḍa* afford protection? Obviously no. It is the *viṣṇusvarūpa* within that protects.

The *śikhā* or tuft is a necessity of a vaiṣṇava ascetic. Sage Śaunaka declares:

`शिखावर्जं ऋतुसन्धिषु वापयेत्। न रोमाणि वापयेत्।`

Hence Vātsya Varadācārya states:

`अतः उपवीतादिवत् शिखापि चतुर्णामपि यतीनाम् अवश्यं धार्या।`[^66]

The sacred thread (*yajñopavīta*) is also very essential to the vaiṣṇava ascetic. Sages Śaunaka, Atri, Hārīta, Vṛddhajābāli and Dattātreya equivocally stress the necessity of donning the sacred thread by the *saṁnyāsin*; and they

[^66]: *Yatiliṅgasamarthana*, p. 10.
[]
declare that all their deeds will bear no fruit if done without *yajñopavīta*, and that atonement (*prāyaścitta*) has to be done for this sin.

Sage Aṅgiras states:

`यतेर्लिङ्गं प्रवक्ष्यामि येनाऽसौ लक्ष्यते यतिः।`
`बहुसूत्रं त्रिदण्डं च वस्त्रं जन्तुनिवारणम्॥`
`शिक्यं पात्रं बृसीश्चैव कौपीनं कटिवेष्टनम्।`
`यथैतद्विद्यते लिङ्गं स यतिर्नेतरो यतिः॥`[^67]

Sage Uśanas declares:

`बहुसूत्रपरित्यागात् ब्रह्मचारी गृही वनी।`
`परिव्राड् वाऽपि पतति तस्मात् तन्न परित्यजेत्॥`[^68]

A *saṁnyāsin* has to perform *śauca* (purificatory functions) like brushing teeth, taking bath etc., just as a householder, but three or four times as much. He is to beg for his food and should eat only once in a day and only a specific quantity. He should eat food not to satisfy himself but only for his sustenance. He should bow to God and other elder ascetics (*vṛddha-yatis*), according to the rules of his order, but he should not pay obeisance to a householder however exalted the latter may be. The ascetic is not to utter any benediction when a person bows to him, but should only utter the name of Lord Nārāyaṇa. He should not mingle freely and should not speak in a flowery language which may attract people:

[^67]: *Yatidharmasamuccaya*, p.13.
[^68]: Ibid. p.12.
[]
`संस्कृतां न वदेद् वाणीं बालवन्मूकवच्चरेत्।`[^69]

In the *Nāradaparivrājakopaniṣad*,[^70] the daily routine of an ascetic (*yaticaryā*) and the fruit thereof (*tatphalam*) are laid down:

`सन्धिष्वे विषयांणां यः समर्थोऽविकलेन्द्रियः।`
`सुप्तवद्वर्तते नित्यं स भिक्षुर्गुम्ध उच्यते॥`
`रागं द्वेषं मदं मायां द्रोहं मोहं परात्मसु।`
`षडेतानि यतिर्नित्यं मनसाऽपि न चिन्तयेत्॥`

When a *saṁnyāsin* dies, he is to be buried in a pit and covered with salt. No mourning is to be observed and no ceremonies (*śrāddhas*) are to be performed except *pārvaṇa* on the eleventh day of his death.

The schism in Śrīvaiṣṇavism has left its marks on the ascetics too. A Vaḍakalai ascetic does not offer obeisance to a householder unless he happens to be the ascetic's own Ācārya. He is not to touch any metallic vessels. He is expected to live only by accepting the specified quantity of food offered to him by orthodox Śrīvaiṣṇava Brahmins. The Teṅgalai ascetic, on the other hand, offers and receives obeisance even from a householder. He need not beg for his food and is allowed the use of metallic vessels of all kinds.[^71] He wears the upper cloth (*uttarīya*) up to the waist and another cloth on the waist.

[^69]: *Śrī Nṛsiṁha Priyā*, August 1992. p.8.
[^70]: III - 68,70.
[^71]: K. Rangachari. *Srīvaiṣṇava Brahmins*, p. 46, Bulletin of Madras Govt. Museum, Vol. II, Madras, 1931.
[]
The Vaḍakalai ascetics wear the cloth only up to the knee and the other cloth should be kept under the arm.

All these are only minor differences and the *Dharmaśāstras* accord the ascetic a pre-eminent position and authority in religious matters. Whenever difficult questions arise about the validity of things from the religious point of view, the political heads generally consult the ascetics. Paul Deussen in his *Philosophy of Upanishads*[^72] observes, "the entire history of mankind does not produce much that approaches in grandeur to this thought enshrined in the *saṁnyāsa dharma*."

### The Maṭhas and Maṭhādhipati-yatis

The Maṭhas or religious institutions, ascetic in nature, were a continuation of an evolving pattern within Hinduism. Brahmanical Hinduism managed to have its way as long as there was no organised opposition to its tenets and way of life. In order to counteract the upsurge of heretical organisations and the onslaughts of Islam and Christianity, Hinduism had to adopt many methods. The Bhakti movement was one, the temple organisation was another, and the religious institutions or Maṭhas were the third. These three provided jointly the socio-religious framework needed to counter heretical religions with a proselytising mission.[^73] There is no basis in Hindu

[^72]: Oriental Publishers, p. 382, New Delhi, 1972.
[^73]: *History of Sri Vaishnavism in the Tamil Country (Post - Rāmānuja)*, p. 149.
[]
Dharma for proselytisation and so there were no institutions for that purpose. The business of the Hindu Maṭhas was rather to conserve and save, regulate, guide and consolidate, than to convert and expand. The numerous discussions which the religious leaders of these institutions held with the leader of the heretical sects were not attempts so much to spread Hinduism as to destroy heresy. So, the Maṭhas were a sort of new defence system to preserve Hinduism.

The Vaiṣṇava Maṭhas were thus a continuation of an evolving pattern within Hinduism. Naturally there were differences in the set up and rules regarding the various Maṭhas – Śaiva Maṭhas of Dharmapuram, Tiruvāvaḍuturai, the Advaita Maṭhas of Śṛṅgerī, Pūrī, Kāñcī and the Śrīvaiṣṇava Maṭha of Ahobilam, Parakāla Maṭha, Śrīmad Āṇḍavan Āśrama, Vānamāmalai Maṭha and so on. One cardinal distinction is that the Śaiva Maṭhas are invariably presided over by the non-Brahmins, especially, "Caturthas" while the Advaita and Viśiṣṭādvaita Maṭhas are headed by Brahmins. The Advaita Maṭhas, on the analogy of Ādi Śaṅkara, are headed by celibates (*naiṣṭhika brahmacārins*) and never by householders or those who had been *gṛhasthas*.[^74]

[^74]: N. Jagadeesan, *History of Sri Vaishnavism in the Tamil Country (Post Rāmānuja)*, p. 150.
[]
The pontifical head (Maṭhādhipati) is primarily an ascetic (*yati*) and in addition he is expected to perform administrative and social duties too. He has to perform five purificatory acts (*pañcasaṁskāra*) for his disciples, receive acts of total surrender (*bharanyāsa*) from whomsoever so desires, propagate Śrī Rāmānuja's philosophy by discourses and by holding meetings of learned scholars (*vidvatsadas*). Hence, even though a *saṁnyāsin* should not acquire wealth, for the sake of the Maṭha, the spiritual head is empowered to raise funds. It is laid down in the *Parāśarasaṁhitā* thus:

`माठपत्यं यतिः कुर्यात् विष्णुधर्मभिवृद्धये।`
`विष्णोर्निर्माल्यसेवाय वेदान्तव्यापनाय च।`
`अन्यथा यः करोत्येवं स याति नरकं ध्रुवम्॥`[^75]

So, the rules are very strict. A *maṭhādhipati yati* should nurture Vaiṣṇava *dharma*, conduct daily worship of the Lord and Bhāgavatas and expound, establish and elaborate Vedāntas through discourses. He has to perform the four-month vow (*cāturmāsyavrata*). This vow should be begun on the *Āṣāḍhapūrṇimā* day. The rainy season begins from this month. During the rainy season, the roads and terrain will be infested with insects, and if the *saṁnyāsin* happens to travel during these months, he may incur the sin of killing these insects and worms. Hence it is ordained in the *śāstras* that the

[^75]: See *Yatidharmasaṅgraha*, p. 133.
[]
*saṁnyāsin* should reside in one place during these four months. The *cāturmāsyavrata saṅkalpa* runs as follows:

`प्रायेण प्रावृषि प्राणि সঙ্কुलं वर्त्म दृश्यते।`
`अतस्तेषां अहिंसार्थं पश्चान् वै श्रुतिसञ्चयान्।`
`स्थास्यामः चतुरो मासान् अत्रैवाऽऽस्तितं बाधके॥`

This *saṅkalpa* is vital for the vow and hence it is that it is termed *saṅkalpamahotsava*. In the Vedas, it is said that a month can be considered to be a fortnight (*pakṣa*) and hence nowadays, the *saṅkalpa* is observed for four fortnights only.[^76]

This *vrata* begins with Viṣvaksena *ārādhana* and then Bhūvarāha *ārādhana* is conducted, followed by prayers offered to Bhūdevī requesting Her for forgiveness for digging the mud. This is collected for bath and cleansing purposes for this period. This is termed *mṛtsaṅgrahaṇam*.

From the mud thus collected, two big balls are offered to the old ascetic reciting a mantra and then they should be taken back from him reciting another mantra, meaning, "May this mud protect me".

Then, as formulated for the saṁnyāsins after *kṣaura, mahānadīsnāna, mādhyāhnika* duties and after worshipping *iṣṭadevatā* and *ācārya*, worship should be done with *kumbha* and chantings of *pañcaśāntimantras*; and then

[^76]: *Śrī Nṛsiṁha Priyā*, p. 11, August 1992.
[]
the holy water should be sprinkled. Then the householders who live nearby should request the *saṁnyāsin* who has performed the *cāturmāsyasaṅkalpa* thus, "Thou shalt reside comfortably in this place. We are grateful to you, and on our part, we will serve you happily." Thus saying, coconuts should be offered to the *saṁnyāsin*.

`निवसन्न्तु सुखेनऽऽत्र गमिष्यामः कृतार्थताम्।`
`यथाशक्ति च शुश्रूषां करिष्यामो वयं मुदा॥`[^77]

After these preliminary rites, worship of the Lord should be performed by the ascetic. During the final celebrations after finishing *kṣaura, mahānadīsnāna* and *anuṣṭhāna*, the remaining mud should be mixed with water and disposed of.[^78]

The Jīyars of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha belong to the *paramahaṁsa* category of ascetics and have been carrying on this *saṁnyāsa* tradition with great fervour. With this, our interest shifts to the Śrīvaiṣṇavite Maṭha of great fame and antiquity - the Ahobila Maṭha and the line of pontiffs or Jīyars who have adorned this sacred Maṭha from its inception in 1398 A.D. to the present day.

[^77]: *Yatidharmasaṅgraha*, p.140.
[^78]: *Śrī Nṛsiṁha Priyā*, p. 12, August 1992.
[]
CHAPTER III
THE JĪYARS OF ŚRĪ AHOBILA MAṬHA
BIOGRAPHICAL DETAILS
[]
## JĪYARS
THE JĪYARS OF ŚRĪ AHOBILA MAṬHA
#### BIOGRAPHICAL DETAILS

This chapter is devoted to the study of the lives of the holy saints who have decorated the Pontificate of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha. Starting with Śrī Ādi Vaṇ Śaṭhakopa till the present Pontiff Śrīman Nārāyaṇa Yatīndra Mahādeśika, 45 men of glory have occupied this position. The relevant biographical details are presented with a view to highlight the social, economic and political background in which they carried out their arduous task. The determination with which each and every Jīyar carried out the divine command of Śrī Lakṣmī Nṛsiṁha by taking the Lord to the nooks and corners of the country in days of yore, when the transport and communication facilities were virtually non-existent, is to be appreciated.

**1. Śrī Ādivaṇ Śaṭhakopa Yatīndra Mahādeśika**

According to the *ŚrīSannidhi-guru-paramparā* also known as *Satsampradāyamuktāvali*,[^79] Ādivaṇ Śaṭhakopa Jīyar, the first Pontiff of Śrī Ahobila Maṭha was the son of Śrī Kiḍāmbi Keśavācārya of Tirunārāyaṇapuram (Melkote) in Karnataka. He was born on Thursday, the fifth day of the bright fortnight (*śuklapakṣapañcamī*) of the solar Tamil month

[^79]: By Śaṭhakopa Yatīndra (33rd Pontiff) : pub. K. Anantacharya, Madras, 1914.
[]
*Puraṭṭāci* in the cyclic year *Siddhārthi* current in the *śaka* year 1301 and *Kali* year 4480, corresponding to September 18, 1379 A.D.

Ādivaṇ Śaṭhakopa Jīyar was named Śrīnivāsa with due reverence to the Lord of the Seven Hills. He received *pañcasaṁskāra* and early secular education at the feet of his father; and Ghaṭikāśatam Varadācārya (known also as Ghaṭikāśatam Ammāḷ[^80] of Kāñcī took charge of his spiritual education. Śrīnivāsa was fully instructed in both Vedāntic and Prabandhic lore and soon was acclaimed as a great exponent of Rāmānuja-darśana.

The event of his life was his dream – the vision of Lord Lakṣmī Narasiṁha of Ahobilam who beckoned him to Ahobilam where he was to renounce the world even as a Brahmacārin, and was later to be the founder of the great Śrīvaiṣṇava Maṭha.
